class="external text" href="http://roalddahl.com">roalddahl<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Roald Dahl</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small>English:  </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/o\xca\x8a/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">o\xca\x8a</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span></span> <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">\xc9\x91\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <small>Norwegian:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Norwegian" title="Help:IPA/Norwegian">[\xcb\x88ru\xcb\x90\xc9\x91l \xcb\x88d\xc9\x91\xcb\x90l]</a></span>; 13 September 1916&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 23 November 1990) was a British novelist, short story writer, poet, screenwriter, and <a href="/wiki/Fighter_pilot" title="Fighter pilot">fighter pilot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> His books have sold more than 250 million copies worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_2016_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2016-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> to Norwegian immigrant parents, Dahl served in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>. He became a <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">flying ace</a> and intelligence officer, rising to the rank of acting <a href="/wiki/Wing_Commander_(rank)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing Commander (rank)">wing commander</a>. He rose to prominence as a writer in the 1940s with works for both children and adults, and he became one of the world\'s best-selling authors.<sup id="cite_ref-Global_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BDC_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BDC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> He has been referred to as "one of the greatest storytellers for children of the 20th century".<sup id="cite_ref-IND_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IND-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> His awards for contribution to literature include the 1983 <a href="/wiki/World_Fantasy_Award_for_Life_Achievement" class="mw-redirect" title="World Fantasy Award for Life Achievement">World Fantasy Award for Life Achievement</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Specsavers_National_Book_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Specsavers National Book Awards">British Book Awards\'</a> Children\'s Author of the Year in 1990. In 2008, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> placed Dahl 16th on its list of "The 50 greatest British writers since 1945".<sup id="cite_ref-TIM_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIM-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl\'s short stories are known for their unexpected endings, and his children\'s books for their unsentimental, macabre, often <a href="/wiki/Black_comedy" title="Black comedy">darkly comic</a> mood, featuring villainous adult enemies of the child characters.<sup id="cite_ref-INT_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INT-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> His books champion the kindhearted, and feature an underlying warm sentiment.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_2015_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2015-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Dahl\'s works for children include <i><a href="/wiki/James_and_the_Giant_Peach" title="James and the Giant Peach">James and the Giant Peach</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory" title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Matilda_(novel)" title="Matilda (novel)">Matilda</a></i>,  <i><a href="/wiki/The_Witches_(novel)" title="The Witches (novel)">The Witches</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Mr_Fox" title="Fantastic Mr Fox">Fantastic Mr Fox</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_BFG" title="The BFG">The BFG</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Twits" title="The Twits">The Twits</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/George%27s_Marvellous_Medicine" title="George&#39;s Marvellous Medicine">George\'s Marvellous Medicine</a></i>. His adult works include <i><a href="/wiki/Tales_of_the_Unexpected_(short_story_collection)" title="Tales of the Unexpected (short story collection)">Tales of the Unexpected</a></i>.\n</p>\n<div class="toctext">Television</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Death_and_legacy"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Death and legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Publications"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Publications</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span></h3>\n<p>Roald Dahl was born in 1916 at Villa Marie, Fairwater Road, in <a href="/wiki/Llandaff" title="Llandaff">Llandaff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a>, Wales, to Norwegian parents, Harald Dahl and Sofie Magdalene Dahl (<i>n\xc3\xa9e</i> Hesselberg).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Dahl\'s father had emigrated to the UK from <a href="/wiki/Sarpsborg" title="Sarpsborg">Sarpsborg</a> in Norway, and settled in Cardiff in the 1880s. His mother came over and married his father in 1911. Dahl was named after the Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Polar_explorer" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar explorer">polar explorer</a> <a href="/wiki/Roald_Amundsen" title="Roald Amundsen">Roald Amundsen</a>. His first language was Norwegian, which he spoke at home with his parents and his sisters Astri, Alfhild and Else. Dahl and his sisters were raised in the <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> faith, and were baptised at the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Church,_Cardiff" title="Norwegian Church, Cardiff">Norwegian Church, Cardiff</a>, where their parents worshipped.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roald_dahl_mrs_pratchetts_sweetshop_llandaff.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Roald_dahl_mrs_pratchetts_sweetshop_llandaff.jpg/220px-Roald_dahl_mrs_pratchetts_sweetshop_llandaff.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Roald_dahl_mrs_pratchetts_sweetshop_llandaff.jpg/330px-Roald_dahl_mrs_pratchetts_sweetshop_llandaff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Roald_dahl_mrs_pratchetts_sweetshop_llandaff.jpg/440px-Roald_dahl_mrs_pratchetts_sweetshop_llandaff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roald_dahl_mrs_pratchetts_sweetshop_llandaff.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mrs Pratchett\'s former sweet shop in Llandaff, Cardiff, has a <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a> commemorating the mischief played by young Roald Dahl and his friends, who were regular customers.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1920, when Dahl was three years old, his seven-year-old sister, Astri, died from <a href="/wiki/Appendicitis" title="Appendicitis">appendicitis</a>. Weeks later, his father died of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> at the age of 57.<sup id="cite_ref-biography_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biography-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> With the option of returning to Norway to live with relatives, Dahl\'s mother decided to remain in Wales. Her husband Harald had wanted their children to be educated in British schools, which he considered the world\'s best.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl first attended the <a href="/wiki/The_Cathedral_School,_Llandaff" title="The Cathedral School, Llandaff">Cathedral School</a>, Llandaff. At the age of eight, he and four of his friends (one named Thwaites) were <a href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment" title="Corporal punishment">caned</a> by the headmaster after putting a dead mouse in a jar of <a href="/wiki/Gobstoppers" class="mw-redirect" title="Gobstoppers">gobstoppers</a> at the local sweet shop,<sup id="cite_ref-IND_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IND-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> which was owned by a "mean and loathsome" old woman called Mrs Pratchett.<sup id="cite_ref-IND_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IND-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The five boys named their prank the "<a href="/wiki/Boy_(book)#Great_mouse_plot_of_1924" title="Boy (book)">Great Mouse Plot of 1924</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Gobstoppers were a favourite sweet among British schoolboys between the two World Wars, and Dahl would refer to them in his creation, <a href="/wiki/Everlasting_Gobstopper" title="Everlasting Gobstopper">Everlasting Gobstopper</a>, which was featured in <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory" title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl transferred to a <a href="/wiki/Boarding_school" title="Boarding school">boarding school</a> in England: St Peter\'s in <a href="/wiki/Weston-super-Mare" title="Weston-super-Mare">Weston-super-Mare</a>. His parents had wanted him to be educated at an <a href="/wiki/Independent_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Independent school (United Kingdom)">English public school</a> and, because of the regular <a href="/wiki/Aust_Ferry" title="Aust Ferry">ferry link</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Channel" title="Bristol Channel">Bristol Channel</a>, this proved to be the nearest. Dahl\'s time at St Peter\'s was unpleasant; he was very homesick and wrote to his mother every week but never revealed his unhappiness to her. After her death in 1967, he learned that she had saved every one of his letters, in small bundles held together with green tape.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, to mark the centenary of Dahl\'s birth, his letters to his mother were abridged and broadcast as BBC Radio 4\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Week" title="Book of the Week">Book of the Week</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Book_of_the_Week_2016_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Book_of_the_Week_2016-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Dahl wrote about his time at St Peter\'s in his autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/Boy_(book)" title="Boy (book)">Boy: Tales of Childhood</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Repton_School">Repton School</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Repton_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1303684.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Repton_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1303684.jpg/220px-Repton_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1303684.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Repton_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1303684.jpg/330px-Repton_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1303684.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Repton_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1303684.jpg/440px-Repton_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1303684.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Repton_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1303684.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dahl attended <a href="/wiki/Repton_School" title="Repton School">Repton School</a> in Derbyshire from 1929 to 1934</div></div></div>\n<p>From 1929, when he was 13, Dahl attended <a href="/wiki/Repton_School" title="Repton School">Repton School</a> in Derbyshire. Dahl disliked the hazing and described an environment of ritual cruelty and status domination, with younger boys having to act as <a href="/wiki/Fagging" title="Fagging">personal servants</a> for older boys, frequently subject to terrible beatings. His biographer Donald Sturrock described these violent experiences in Dahl\'s early life.<sup id="cite_ref-Dahl_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dahl-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Dahl expresses some of these darker experiences in his writings, which is also marked by his hatred of cruelty and <a href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment" title="Corporal punishment">corporal punishment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/Boy_(book)" title="Boy (book)">Boy: Tales of Childhood</a></i>, a friend named Michael was viciously caned by headmaster <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Fisher" title="Geoffrey Fisher">Geoffrey Fisher</a>. Writing in that same book, Dahl reflected: \xe2\x80\x9cAll through my school life I was appalled by the fact that masters and senior boys were allowed literally to wound other boys, and sometimes quite severely... I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get over it. I never have got over it.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The master was later selected as the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">crowned</a> <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> in 1953. (However, according to Dahl\'s biographer <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Treglown" title="Jeremy Treglown">Jeremy Treglown</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> the caning took place in May 1933, a year after Fisher had left Repton; the headmaster was in fact <a href="/wiki/John_Traill_Christie" class="mw-redirect" title="John Traill Christie">J. T. Christie</a>, Fisher\'s successor.) Dahl said the incident caused him to "have doubts about religion and even about God".<sup id="cite_ref-boy_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boy-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>He was never seen as a particularly talented writer in his school years, with one of his English teachers writing in his school report "I have never met anybody who so persistently writes words meaning the exact opposite of what is intended."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Dahl was exceptionally tall, reaching 6&#160;feet 6&#160;inches (1.98&#160;m) in adult life.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> He played a number of sports including cricket, <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> and golf, and was made captain of the <a href="/wiki/Squash_(sport)" title="Squash (sport)">squash</a> team.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> As well as having a passion for literature, he developed an interest in photography and often carried a camera with him.<sup id="cite_ref-biography_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biography-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During his years at Repton, the <a href="/wiki/Cadbury" title="Cadbury">Cadbury</a> chocolate company would occasionally send boxes of new chocolates to the school to be tested by the pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Dahl would dream of inventing a new chocolate bar that would win the praise of <a href="/wiki/Cadbury_family" title="Cadbury family">Mr Cadbury</a> himself; this inspired him in writing his third children\'s book, <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory" title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a></i> (1964), and to refer to chocolate in other children\'s books.<sup id="cite_ref-DahlChocolate_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DahlChocolate-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout his childhood and adolescent years, Dahl spent the majority of his summer holidays with his mother\'s family in Norway. He wrote about many happy memories from those visits in <i>Boy: Tales of Childhood</i>, such as when he replaced the tobacco in his half\xe2\x80\x93sister\'s fianc\xc3\xa9\'s pipe with goat droppings.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> He noted only one unhappy memory of his holidays in Norway: at around the age of eight, he had to have his <a href="/wiki/Adenoid" title="Adenoid">adenoids</a> removed by a doctor.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> His childhood and first job selling kerosene in <a href="/wiki/Midsomer_Norton" title="Midsomer Norton">Midsomer Norton</a> and surrounding villages in <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a> are subjects in <i>Boy: Tales of Childhood</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_school">After school</span></h3>\n<p>After finishing his schooling, in August 1934 Dahl crossed the Atlantic on the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Nova_Scotia_(1926)" title="RMS Nova Scotia (1926)">RMS&#160;<i>Nova Scotia</i></a> and hiked through <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> with the <a href="/wiki/BSES_Expeditions" class="mw-redirect" title="BSES Expeditions">Public Schools Exploring Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 1934, Dahl joined the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Shell Petroleum</a> Company. Following two years of training in the United Kingdom, he was assigned first to <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, Kenya, then to <a href="/wiki/Dar-es-Salaam" class="mw-redirect" title="Dar-es-Salaam">Dar-es-Salaam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika" title="Tanganyika">Tanganyika</a> (now Tanzania). Along with the only two other Shell employees in the entire territory, he lived in luxury in the Shell House outside Dar es Salaam, with a cook and personal servants. While out on assignments supplying oil to customers across Tanganyika, he encountered <a href="/wiki/Black_mamba" title="Black mamba">black mambas</a> and lions, among other wildlife.<sup id="cite_ref-boy_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boy-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fighter_ace">Fighter ace</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roald_Dahl%27s_leather_flying_helmet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Roald_Dahl%27s_leather_flying_helmet.jpg/220px-Roald_Dahl%27s_leather_flying_helmet.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Roald_Dahl%27s_leather_flying_helmet.jpg/330px-Roald_Dahl%27s_leather_flying_helmet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Roald_Dahl%27s_leather_flying_helmet.jpg/440px-Roald_Dahl%27s_leather_flying_helmet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3611" data-file-height="2950" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roald_Dahl%27s_leather_flying_helmet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dahl\'s leather flying helmet on display in the <a href="/wiki/Roald_Dahl_Museum_and_Story_Centre" title="Roald Dahl Museum and Story Centre">Roald Dahl Museum and Story Centre</a> in Great Missenden</div></div></div>\n<p>In August 1939, as the Second World War loomed, the British made plans to round up the hundreds of Germans living in <a href="/wiki/Dar-es-Salaam" class="mw-redirect" title="Dar-es-Salaam">Dar-es-Salaam</a>. Dahl was commissioned as a <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_(British_Army_and_Royal_Marines)" title="Lieutenant (British Army and Royal Marines)">lieutenant</a> into the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_African_Rifles" title="King&#39;s African Rifles">King\'s African Rifles</a>, commanding a platoon of <a href="/wiki/Askari" title="Askari">Askari</a> men, indigenous troops who were serving in the colonial army.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1939, Dahl joined the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Aircraftman" title="Aircraftman">aircraftman</a> with service number 774022.<sup id="cite_ref-LG5907_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LG5907-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> After a 600-mile (970&#160;km) car journey from Dar es Salaam to <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, he was accepted for flight training with sixteen other men; only three others of these survived the war. With seven hours and 40 minutes experience in a <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland_Tiger_Moth" title="De Havilland Tiger Moth">De Havilland Tiger Moth</a>, he flew solo;<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Dahl enjoyed watching the wildlife of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> during his flights. He continued to advanced flying training in Iraq, at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Habbaniya" title="RAF Habbaniya">RAF Habbaniya</a>, 50 miles (80&#160;km) west of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>. Following six months\' training on <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hart" title="Hawker Hart">Hawker Harts</a>, Dahl was commissioned as a <a href="/wiki/Pilot_officer" title="Pilot officer">pilot officer</a> on 24 August 1940, and was judged ready to join a squadron and face the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-LG5907_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LG5907-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gloster_Gladiator.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Gloster_Gladiator.jpg/220px-Gloster_Gladiator.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Gloster_Gladiator.jpg/330px-Gloster_Gladiator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Gloster_Gladiator.jpg/440px-Gloster_Gladiator.jpg 2x" data-file-width="747" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gloster_Gladiator.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dahl was flying a <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Gladiator" title="Gloster Gladiator">Gloster Gladiator</a> when he crash landed in Libya</div></div></div>\n<p>He was assigned to <a href="/wiki/No._80_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 80 Squadron RAF">No. 80 Squadron RAF</a>, flying obsolete <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Gladiator" title="Gloster Gladiator">Gloster Gladiators</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Biplane" title="Biplane">biplane</a> <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter aircraft</a> used by the RAF. Dahl was surprised to find that he would not receive any specialised training in <a href="/wiki/Aerial_combat" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerial combat">aerial combat</a>, or in flying Gladiators. On 19 September 1940, Dahl was ordered to fly his Gladiator by stages from Abu Sueir (near <a href="/wiki/Ismailia" title="Ismailia">Ismailia</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>) to 80 Squadron\'s forward <a href="/wiki/Airstrip" class="mw-redirect" title="Airstrip">airstrip</a> 30 miles (48&#160;km) south of <a href="/wiki/Mersa_Matruh" title="Mersa Matruh">Mersa Matruh</a>. On the final leg he could not find the airstrip and, running low on <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> and with night approaching, he was forced to attempt a <a href="/wiki/Accidents_and_incidents_in_aviation#Causes" class="mw-redirect" title="Accidents and incidents in aviation">landing</a> in the desert.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The undercarriage hit a boulder and the aircraft crashed. Dahl\'s skull was fractured and his nose was smashed; he was temporarily blinded.<sup id="cite_ref-WRN_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WRN-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> He managed to drag himself away from the blazing wreckage and passed out. He wrote about the crash in his first published work.<sup id="cite_ref-WRN_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WRN-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl was rescued and taken to a <a href="/wiki/First_aid" title="First aid">first-aid</a> post in Mersa Matruh, where he regained consciousness, but not his sight. He was transported by train to the Royal Navy hospital in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>. There he fell in and out of love with a nurse, Mary Welland. A RAF inquiry into the crash revealed that the location to which he had been told to fly was completely wrong, and he had mistakenly been sent instead to the <a href="/wiki/No_man%27s_land" title="No man&#39;s land">no man\'s land</a> between the Allied and Italian forces.<sup id="cite_ref-solo_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solo-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg/220px-Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg/330px-Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg/440px-Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2308" data-file-height="1501" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_mk1_r4118_fairford_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane" title="Hawker Hurricane">Hawker Hurricane Mk 1</a>, the aircraft type in which Dahl engaged in aerial combat over Greece.</div></div></div>\n<p>In February 1941, Dahl was discharged from hospital and passed fully fit for flying duties. By this time, 80 Squadron had been transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Greece" title="Battle of Greece">Greek campaign</a> and based at <a href="/wiki/Eleusina" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleusina">Eleusina</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>. The squadron was now equipped with <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane" title="Hawker Hurricane">Hawker Hurricanes</a>. Dahl flew a replacement Hurricane across the Mediterranean Sea in April 1941, after seven hours\' experience flying Hurricanes. By this stage in the Greek campaign, the RAF had only 18 combat aircraft in Greece: 14 Hurricanes and four <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Blenheim" title="Bristol Blenheim">Bristol Blenheim</a> light bombers. Dahl flew in his first aerial combat on 15 April 1941, while flying alone over the city of <a href="/wiki/Chalcis" title="Chalcis">Chalcis</a>. He attacked six <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_88" title="Junkers Ju 88">Junkers Ju 88s</a> that were bombing ships and shot one down. On 16 April in another air battle, he shot down another Ju 88.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 20 April 1941, Dahl took part in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Athens_(1941)" title="Battle of Athens (1941)">Battle of Athens</a>, alongside the highest-scoring British Commonwealth ace of World War II, <a href="/wiki/Pat_Pattle" title="Pat Pattle">Pat Pattle</a>, and Dahl\'s friend <a href="/wiki/David_Coke" title="David Coke">David Coke</a>. Of 12 Hurricanes involved, five were shot down and four of their pilots killed, including Pattle. Greek observers on the ground counted 22 German aircraft downed, but because of the confusion of the aerial engagement, none of the pilots knew which aircraft they had shot down. Dahl described it as "an endless blur of enemy fighters whizzing towards me from every side".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May, as the Germans were pressing on Athens, Dahl was evacuated to Egypt. His squadron was reassembled in <a href="/wiki/Haifa" title="Haifa">Haifa</a>. From there, Dahl flew sorties every day for a period of four weeks, shooting down a <a href="/wiki/Vichy_French_Air_Force" title="Vichy French Air Force">Vichy French Air Force</a> <a href="/wiki/Potez_63" class="mw-redirect" title="Potez 63">Potez 63</a> on 8 June and another Ju 88 on 15 June, but he began to get severe headaches that caused him to black out. He was invalided home to Britain. Though at this time Dahl was only a pilot officer on probation, in September 1941 he was simultaneously confirmed as a <a href="/wiki/Pilot_officer" title="Pilot officer">pilot officer</a> and promoted to war substantive <a href="/wiki/Flying_officer" title="Flying officer">flying officer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Diplomat.2C_writer_and_intelligence_officer"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomat,_writer_and_intelligence_officer">Diplomat, writer and intelligence officer</span></h2>\n<p>After being invalided home, Dahl was posted to an RAF training camp in <a href="/wiki/Uxbridge" title="Uxbridge">Uxbridge</a>. He attempted to recover his health enough to become an instructor.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In late March 1942, while in London, he met the <a href="/wiki/Under-Secretary_of_State_for_Air" title="Under-Secretary of State for Air">Under-Secretary of State for Air</a>, Major Harold Balfour (later <a href="/wiki/Harold_Balfour,_1st_Baron_Balfour_of_Inchrye" title="Harold Balfour, 1st Baron Balfour of Inchrye">Lord Balfour</a>), at his club. Impressed by Dahl\'s war record and conversational abilities, Balfour appointed the young man as assistant air attach\xc3\xa9 at the <a href="/wiki/British_Embassy" class="mw-redirect" title="British Embassy">British Embassy</a> in Washington, D.C. Initially resistant, Dahl was finally persuaded by Balfour to accept, and took passage on the SS <i>Batori</i> from Glasgow a few days later. He arrived in Halifax, Canada, on 14 April, after which he took a sleeper train to <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Coming from war-starved Britain, Dahl was amazed by the wealth of food and amenities to be had in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Arriving in Washington a week later, Dahl found he liked the atmosphere of the U.S. capital. He shared a house with another attach\xc3\xa9 at 1610 34th Street, NW, in <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Georgetown (Washington, D.C.)">Georgetown</a>. But after ten days in his new posting, Dahl strongly disliked it, feeling he had taken on "a most ungodly unimportant job."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> He later explained, "I\'d just come from the war. People were getting killed. I had been flying around, seeing horrible things. Now, almost instantly, I found myself in the middle of a pre-war cocktail party in America."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl was unimpressed by his office in the British Air Mission, attached to the embassy. He was also unimpressed by the ambassador, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Wood,_1st_Earl_of_Halifax" title="Edward Wood, 1st Earl of Halifax">Lord Halifax</a>, with whom he sometimes played tennis and whom he described as "a courtly English gentleman". Dahl socialized with Charles E. Marsh, a Texas publisher and oilman, at his house at 2136 R Street, NW, and the Marsh country estate in Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-solo_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solo-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dietsch_2013_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dietsch_2013-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> As part of his duties as assistant air attach\xc3\xa9, Dahl was to help neutralise the isolationist views still held by many Americans by giving pro-British speeches and discussing his war service; the United States had entered the war only the previous December, following the attack on <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Irreg_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Irreg-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At this time Dahl met the noted British novelist <a href="/wiki/C._S._Forester" title="C. S. Forester">C. S. Forester</a>, who was also working to aid the British war effort. Forester worked for the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Information_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Information (United Kingdom)">British Ministry of Information</a> and was writing propaganda for the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> cause, mainly for American consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Saturday Evening Post</i> had asked Forester to write a story based on Dahl\'s flying experiences; Forester asked Dahl to write down some RAF anecdotes so that he could shape them into a story. After Forester read what Dahl had given him, he decided to publish the story exactly as Dahl had written it.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Studios_2016_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Studios_2016-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> He originally titled the article as "A Piece of Cake" but the magazine changed it to "Shot Down Over Libya" to make it sound more dramatic, although Dahl had not been shot down; it was published in the 1 August 1942 issue of the <i>Post</i>. Dahl was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Flight_lieutenant" title="Flight lieutenant">flight lieutenant</a> (war-substantive) in August 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  Later he worked with such other well-known British officers as <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming" title="Ian Fleming">Ian Fleming</a> (who later published the popular <i>James Bond</i> series) and <a href="/wiki/David_Ogilvy_(businessman)" title="David Ogilvy (businessman)">David Ogilvy</a>, promoting Britain\'s interests and message in the U.S. and combating the "<a href="/wiki/America_First_Committee" title="America First Committee">America First</a>" movement.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Irreg_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Irreg-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This work introduced Dahl to espionage and the activities of the Canadian spymaster <a href="/wiki/William_Stephenson" title="William Stephenson">William Stephenson</a>, known by the codename "Intrepid".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> During the war, Dahl supplied intelligence from Washington to Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>. As Dahl later said: "My job was to try to help Winston to get on with <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">FDR</a>, and tell Winston what was in the old boy\'s mind."<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Studios_2016_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Studios_2016-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Dahl also supplied intelligence to Stephenson and his organisation, known as <a href="/wiki/British_Security_Coordination" class="mw-redirect" title="British Security Coordination">British Security Coordination</a>, which was part of <a href="/wiki/Secret_Intelligence_Service" title="Secret Intelligence Service">MI6</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dietsch_2013_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dietsch_2013-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Dahl was once sent back to Britain by British Embassy officials, supposedly for misconduct\xe2\x80\x94"I got booted out by the big boys," he said. Stephenson promptly sent him back to Washington\xe2\x80\x94with a promotion to <a href="/wiki/Wing_Commander_(rank)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing Commander (rank)">wing commander</a> rank.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Toward the end of the war, Dahl wrote some of the history of the secret organisation; he and Stephenson remained friends for decades after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upon the war\'s conclusion, Dahl held the rank of a temporary <a href="/wiki/Wing_Commander_(rank)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing Commander (rank)">wing commander</a> (substantive flight lieutenant). Owing to the severity of his injuries from the 1940 accident, he was pronounced unfit for further service and was invalided out of the RAF in August 1946. He left the service with the substantive rank of <a href="/wiki/Squadron_leader" title="Squadron leader">squadron leader</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> His record of five aerial victories, qualifying him as a <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">flying ace</a>, has been confirmed by post-war research and cross-referenced in Axis records. It is most likely that he scored more than those victories during 20 April 1941, when 22 German aircraft were shot down.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_life">Post-war life</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Patricia_Neal_und_Roald_Dahl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Patricia_Neal_und_Roald_Dahl.jpg/200px-Patricia_Neal_und_Roald_Dahl.jpg" width="200" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Patricia_Neal_und_Roald_Dahl.jpg/300px-Patricia_Neal_und_Roald_Dahl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Patricia_Neal_und_Roald_Dahl.jpg/400px-Patricia_Neal_und_Roald_Dahl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="664" data-file-height="936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Patricia_Neal_und_Roald_Dahl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Patricia_Neal" title="Patricia Neal">Patricia Neal</a> and Roald Dahl</div></div></div>\n<p>Dahl married American actress <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Neal" title="Patricia Neal">Patricia Neal</a> on 2 July 1953 at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Church,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity Church, New York">Trinity Church</a> in New York City. Their marriage lasted for 30 years and they had five children: \n</p>\n<ul><li>Olivia Twenty (20 April 1955 \xe2\x80\x93 17 November 1962);</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tessa_Dahl" title="Tessa Dahl">Chantal Sophia "Tessa"</a> (born 1957), who became an author, and mother of author, cookbook writer and former model <a href="/wiki/Sophie_Dahl" title="Sophie Dahl">Sophie Dahl</a> (after whom Sophie in <i><a href="/wiki/The_BFG" title="The BFG">The BFG</a></i> is named).;<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Theo Matthew (born 1960);</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ophelia_Dahl" title="Ophelia Dahl">Ophelia Magdalena</a> (born 1964);</li>\n<li>and <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Dahl" title="Lucy Dahl">Lucy Neal</a> (born 1965).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>On 5 December 1960, four-month-old Theo Dahl was severely injured when his baby carriage was struck by a taxicab in New York City. For a time, he suffered from <a href="/wiki/Hydrocephalus" title="Hydrocephalus">hydrocephalus</a>. As a result, his father became involved in the development of what became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Wade-Dahl-Till_valve" title="Wade-Dahl-Till valve">Wade-Dahl-Till</a>" (or WDT) valve, a device to alleviate the condition.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-larner_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-larner-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The valve was a collaboration between Dahl, hydraulic engineer Stanley Wade, and London\'s <a href="/wiki/Great_Ormond_Street_Hospital" title="Great Ormond Street Hospital">Great Ormond Street Hospital</a> neurosurgeon Kenneth Till, and was used successfully on almost 3,000 children around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Olivia_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olivia-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1962, Dahl\'s daughter Olivia died of <a href="/wiki/Subacute_sclerosing_panencephalitis" title="Subacute sclerosing panencephalitis">measles encephalitis</a>, age seven. Her death left Dahl "limp with despair", and feeling guilty about not having been able to do anything for her.<sup id="cite_ref-Olivia_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olivia-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Dahl subsequently became a proponent of <a href="/wiki/Immunisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Immunisation">immunisation</a> and dedicated his 1982 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_BFG" title="The BFG">The BFG</a></i> to his daughter.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> After Olivia\'s death and a meeting with a Church official, Dahl came to view Christianity as a sham.<sup id="cite_ref-faith_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faith-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> While mourning her loss, he had sought spiritual guidance from <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Fisher" title="Geoffrey Fisher">Geoffrey Fisher</a>, the former Archbishop of Canterbury. He was dismayed by Fisher telling him that, although Olivia was in Paradise, her beloved dog Rowley would never join her there.<sup id="cite_ref-faith_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faith-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Dahl recalled years later: \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to ask him how he could be so absolutely sure that other creatures did not get the same special treatment as us. I sat there wondering if this great and famous churchman really knew what he was talking about and whether he knew anything at all about God or heaven, and if he didn\'t, then who in the world did?<sup id="cite_ref-faith_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faith-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1965, his wife Patricia Neal suffered three burst <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_aneurysm" class="mw-redirect" title="Cerebral aneurysm">cerebral aneurysms</a> while pregnant with their fifth child, Lucy. Dahl took control of her rehabilitation over the next months; Neal had to re-learn to talk and walk, but she managed to return to her acting career.<sup id="cite_ref-farrell_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-farrell-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> This period of their lives was dramatised in the film <i>The Patricia Neal Story</i> (1981), in which the couple were played by <a href="/wiki/Glenda_Jackson" title="Glenda Jackson">Glenda Jackson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dirk_Bogarde" title="Dirk Bogarde">Dirk Bogarde</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roald_Dahl_signeert_boeken_in_de_Kinderboekenwinkel_in_Amsterdam,_Bestanddeelnr_934-3367.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Roald_Dahl_signeert_boeken_in_de_Kinderboekenwinkel_in_Amsterdam%2C_Bestanddeelnr_934-3367.jpg/220px-Roald_Dahl_signeert_boeken_in_de_Kinderboekenwinkel_in_Amsterdam%2C_Bestanddeelnr_934-3367.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Roald_Dahl_signeert_boeken_in_de_Kinderboekenwinkel_in_Amsterdam%2C_Bestanddeelnr_934-3367.jpg/330px-Roald_Dahl_signeert_boeken_in_de_Kinderboekenwinkel_in_Amsterdam%2C_Bestanddeelnr_934-3367.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Roald_Dahl_signeert_boeken_in_de_Kinderboekenwinkel_in_Amsterdam%2C_Bestanddeelnr_934-3367.jpg/440px-Roald_Dahl_signeert_boeken_in_de_Kinderboekenwinkel_in_Amsterdam%2C_Bestanddeelnr_934-3367.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3683" data-file-height="2445" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roald_Dahl_signeert_boeken_in_de_Kinderboekenwinkel_in_Amsterdam,_Bestanddeelnr_934-3367.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dahl signing books in Amsterdam, Netherlands, October 1988</div></div></div>\n<p>Neal and Dahl divorced in 1983. He married <a href="/wiki/Clare_McLaren-Throckmorton#Personal_life" title="Clare McLaren-Throckmorton">Felicity "Liccy" Crosland</a>, niece of Francis D\'Abreu who was married to Margaret Ann Bowes Lyon - 1st cousin of the late Queen Mother, at <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Lambeth#Landmarks" title="London Borough of Lambeth">Brixton Town Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_London" title="South London">South London</a>. Dahl and Crosland had previously been in a relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Liccy gave up her job and moved into "Gipsy House", <a href="/wiki/Great_Missenden" title="Great Missenden">Great Missenden</a> in <a href="/wiki/Buckinghamshire" title="Buckinghamshire">Buckinghamshire</a>, which had been Dahl\'s home since 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1983 Dahl reviewed Tony Clifton\'s <i>God Cried</i>, a picture book about the siege of West Beirut by the Israeli army during the <a href="/wiki/1982_Lebanon_War" title="1982 Lebanon War">1982 Lebanon War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> He wrote that the book would make readers "violently anti-Israeli", saying, "I am not anti-Semitic. I am anti-Israel."<sup id="cite_ref-treg_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treg-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Dahl told a reporter in 1983, "There\'s a trait in the Jewish character that does provoke animosity ... I mean there is always a reason why anti-anything crops up anywhere; even a stinker like Hitler didn\'t just pick on them for no reason."<sup id="cite_ref-treg_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treg-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Dahl had Jewish friends, including philosopher Sir <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_Berlin" title="Isaiah Berlin">Isaiah Berlin</a>, who said, "I thought he might say anything. Could have been pro-Arab or pro-Jew. There was no consistent line. He was a man who followed whims, which meant he would blow up in one direction, so to speak."<sup id="cite_ref-treg_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treg-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Amelia Foster, director of the Roald Dahl Museum in Great Missenden, says, "This is again an example of how Dahl refused to take anything seriously, even himself. He was very angry at the Israelis. He had a childish reaction to what was going on in Israel. Dahl wanted to provoke, as he always provoked at dinner. His publisher was a Jew, his agent was a Jew... and he thought nothing but good things of them. He asked me to be his managing director, and I\'m Jewish."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1986 <a href="/wiki/New_Years_Honours_List" class="mw-redirect" title="New Years Honours List">New Years Honours List</a>, Dahl was offered an appointment to <a href="/wiki/Officer_of_the_Order_of_the_British_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Officer of the Order of the British Empire">Officer of the Order of the British Empire</a> (OBE), but turned it down. He reportedly wanted a <a href="/wiki/Orders,_decorations,_and_medals_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Orders, decorations, and medals of the United Kingdom">knighthood</a> so that his wife would be Lady Dahl.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, Dahl was featured in the list of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Elizabethans" title="The New Elizabethans">The New Elizabethans</a></i> to mark the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamond Jubilee of Queen Elizabeth II">diamond Jubilee of Queen Elizabeth II</a>.  A panel of seven academics, journalists and historians named Dahl among the group of people in the UK "whose actions during the reign of Elizabeth II have had a significant impact on lives in these islands and given the age its character".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Deviousdahl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9c/Deviousdahl.jpg/220px-Deviousdahl.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9c/Deviousdahl.jpg/330px-Deviousdahl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/9c/Deviousdahl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="393" data-file-height="253" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Deviousdahl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roald Dahl\'s story "The Devious Bachelor" was illustrated by Frederick Siebel when it was published in <i><a href="/wiki/Collier%27s_Weekly" class="mw-redirect" title="Collier&#39;s Weekly">Collier\'s</a></i> (September 1953).</div></div></div>\n<p>Dahl\'s first published work, inspired by a meeting with <a href="/wiki/C._S._Forester" title="C. S. Forester">C. S. Forester</a>, was "A Piece of Cake", on 1 August 1942. The story, about his wartime adventures, was bought by <i>The Saturday Evening Post</i> for US$1,000 (a substantial sum in 1942) and published under the title "Shot Down Over Libya".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His first children\'s book was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gremlins" title="The Gremlins">The Gremlins</a></i>, published in 1943, about mischievous little creatures that were part of Royal Air Force folklore.<sup id="cite_ref-dong_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dong-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The RAF pilots blamed the <a href="/wiki/Gremlin" title="Gremlin">gremlins</a> for all the problems with the aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> While at the British Embassy in Washington, Dahl sent a copy to the <a href="/wiki/First_Lady" title="First Lady">First Lady</a> <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a> who read it to her grandchildren,<sup id="cite_ref-dong_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dong-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and the book was commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> for a film that was never made.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Dahl went on to create some of the best-loved children\'s stories of the 20th century, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory" title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Matilda_(novel)" title="Matilda (novel)">Matilda</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/James_and_the_Giant_Peach" title="James and the Giant Peach">James and the Giant Peach</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Witches_(novel)" title="The Witches (novel)">The Witches</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Mr_Fox" title="Fantastic Mr Fox">Fantastic Mr Fox</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_BFG" title="The BFG">The BFG</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Twits" title="The Twits">The Twits</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/George%27s_Marvellous_Medicine" title="George&#39;s Marvellous Medicine">George\'s Marvellous Medicine</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-IND_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IND-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl also had a successful parallel career as the writer of macabre adult short stories, usually with a dark sense of humour and a surprise ending.<sup id="cite_ref-arwd_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arwd-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mystery_Writers_of_America" title="Mystery Writers of America">Mystery Writers of America</a> presented Dahl with three <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar Awards">Edgar Awards</a> for his work, and many were originally written for American magazines such as <i><a href="/wiki/Collier%27s_Weekly" class="mw-redirect" title="Collier&#39;s Weekly">Collier\'s</a></i> ("The Collector\'s Item" was <i>Collier\'s</i> Star Story of the week for 4 September 1948), <i><a href="/wiki/Ladies_Home_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Ladies Home Journal">Ladies Home Journal</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Magazine" title="Harper&#39;s Magazine">Harper\'s</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Works such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kiss_Kiss_(book)" title="Kiss Kiss (book)">Kiss Kiss</a></i> subsequently collected Dahl\'s stories into anthologies, gaining worldwide acclaim. Dahl wrote more than 60 short stories; they have appeared in numerous collections, some only being published in book form after his death (see <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roald_Dahl_short_stories" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Roald Dahl short stories">List of Roald Dahl short stories</a>). His three Edgar Awards were given for: in 1954, the collection <i>Someone Like You</i>; in 1959, the story "<a href="/wiki/The_Landlady_(Roald_Dahl)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Landlady (Roald Dahl)">The Landlady</a>"; and in 1980, the episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Tales_of_the_Unexpected_(TV_series)" title="Tales of the Unexpected (TV series)">Tales of the Unexpected</a></i> based on "<a href="/wiki/Skin_(short_story)" title="Skin (short story)">Skin</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-arwd_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arwd-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roald_Dahl%27s_gipsy_caravan_-_geograph.org.uk_-_112566.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Roald_Dahl%27s_gipsy_caravan_-_geograph.org.uk_-_112566.jpg/220px-Roald_Dahl%27s_gipsy_caravan_-_geograph.org.uk_-_112566.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Roald_Dahl%27s_gipsy_caravan_-_geograph.org.uk_-_112566.jpg/330px-Roald_Dahl%27s_gipsy_caravan_-_geograph.org.uk_-_112566.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Roald_Dahl%27s_gipsy_caravan_-_geograph.org.uk_-_112566.jpg/440px-Roald_Dahl%27s_gipsy_caravan_-_geograph.org.uk_-_112566.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="473" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roald_Dahl%27s_gipsy_caravan_-_geograph.org.uk_-_112566.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roald Dahl\'s <a href="/wiki/Vardo_(Romani_wagon)" title="Vardo (Romani wagon)">vardo</a> in the garden of his house, "Gipsy Cottage", in Great Missenden, where he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Danny,_the_Champion_of_the_World" title="Danny, the Champion of the World">Danny, the Champion of the World</a></i> in 1975.</div></div></div>\n<p>One of his more famous adult stories, "The Smoker", also known as "<a href="/wiki/Man_from_the_South" title="Man from the South">Man from the South</a>", was filmed twice as both 1960 and 1985 episodes of <i><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock_Presents" title="Alfred Hitchcock Presents">Alfred Hitchcock Presents</a></i>, and also adapted into <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Tarantino" title="Quentin Tarantino">Quentin Tarantino</a>\'s segment of the 1995 film <i><a href="/wiki/Four_Rooms" title="Four Rooms">Four Rooms</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-quet_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quet-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> This oft-anthologised classic concerns a man in Jamaica who wagers with visitors in an attempt to claim the fingers from their hands. The 1960 Hitchcock version stars <a href="/wiki/Steve_McQueen_(actor)" class="mw-redirect" title="Steve McQueen (actor)">Steve McQueen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lorre" title="Peter Lorre">Peter Lorre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-quet_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quet-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl acquired a traditional <a href="/wiki/Romanichal" title="Romanichal">Romanichal</a> <a href="/wiki/Vardo_(Romani_wagon)" title="Vardo (Romani wagon)">vardo</a> in the 1960s, and the family used it as a playhouse for his children at home in <a href="/wiki/Great_Missenden" title="Great Missenden">Great Missenden</a>, Buckinghamshire. He later used the vardo as a writing room, where he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Danny,_the_Champion_of_the_World" title="Danny, the Champion of the World">Danny, the Champion of the World</a></i> in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Dahl incorporated a Gypsy wagon into the main plot of the book, where the young English boy, Danny, and his father, William (played by <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Irons" title="Jeremy Irons">Jeremy Irons</a> in the film adaptation) live in a vardo.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Many other scenes and characters from Great Missenden are reflected in his work. For example the village library was the inspiration for Mrs Phelps\' library in <i>Matilda</i>, where the title character devours classic literature by the age of four.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His short story collection <i>Tales of the Unexpected</i> was adapted to a successful <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_the_Unexpected_(TV_series)" title="Tales of the Unexpected (TV series)">TV series of the same name</a>, beginning with <i>Man From the South</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-totu_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totu-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> When the stock of Dahl\'s own original stories was exhausted, the series continued by adapting stories by authors that were written in Dahl\'s style, including the writers <a href="/wiki/John_Collier_(fiction_writer)" title="John Collier (fiction writer)">John Collier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Ellin" title="Stanley Ellin">Stanley Ellin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some of his short stories are supposed to be extracts from the diary of his (fictional) Uncle Oswald, a rich gentleman whose sexual exploits form the subject of these stories.<sup id="cite_ref-OSW_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSW-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In his novel <i><a href="/wiki/My_Uncle_Oswald" title="My Uncle Oswald">My Uncle Oswald</a></i>, the uncle engages a temptress to seduce 20th century geniuses and royalty with a love potion secretly added to chocolate truffles made by Dahl\'s favourite chocolate shop, <a href="/wiki/Prestat" title="Prestat">Prestat</a> of Piccadilly, London.<sup id="cite_ref-OSW_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSW-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Memories_with_Food_at_Gipsy_House" title="Memories with Food at Gipsy House">Memories with Food at Gipsy House</a></i>, written with his wife Felicity and published posthumously in 1991, was a mixture of recipes, family reminiscences and Dahl\'s musings on favourite subjects such as chocolate, onions and claret.<sup id="cite_ref-TELG_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TELG-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Children.27s_fiction"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Children\'s_fiction">Children\'s fiction</span></h3>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:28%;&#32;;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"He [Dahl] was mischievous. A grown-up being mischievous. He addresses you, a child, as somebody who knows about the world. He was a grown-up \xe2\x80\x93 and he was bigger than most \xe2\x80\x93 who is on your side. That must have something to do with it."</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">\xe2\x80\x94Illustrator <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Blake" title="Quentin Blake">Quentin Blake</a> on the lasting appeal of Dahl\'s children\'s books.<sup id="cite_ref-IND_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IND-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Dahl\'s children\'s works are usually told from the point of view of a child. They typically involve adult <a href="/wiki/Villain" title="Villain">villains</a> who hate and mistreat children, and feature at least one "good" adult to counteract the villain(s).<sup id="cite_ref-IND_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IND-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> These stock characters are possibly a reference to the abuse that Dahl stated that he experienced in the <a href="/wiki/Boarding_school" title="Boarding school">boarding schools</a> he attended.<sup id="cite_ref-IND_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IND-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Dahl\'s books see the triumph of the child; children\'s book critic Amanda Craig said, "He was unequivocal that it is the good, young and kind who triumph over the old, greedy and the wicked."<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_2015_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2015-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> While his whimsical fantasy stories feature an underlying warm sentiment, they usually contain a lot of <a href="/wiki/Black_comedy" title="Black comedy">darkly comic</a> and grotesque scenarios, including gruesome violence.<sup id="cite_ref-INT_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INT-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Witches_(novel)" title="The Witches (novel)">The Witches</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/George%27s_Marvellous_Medicine" title="George&#39;s Marvellous Medicine">George\'s Marvellous Medicine</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Matilda_(novel)" title="Matilda (novel)">Matilda</a></i> are examples of this formula. <i><a href="/wiki/The_BFG" title="The BFG">The BFG</a></i> follows it in a more analogous way with the good giant (the BFG or "Big Friendly Giant") representing the "good adult" archetype and the other giants being the "bad adults". This formula is also somewhat evident in Dahl\'s film script for <i><a href="/wiki/Chitty_Chitty_Bang_Bang_(film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chitty Chitty Bang Bang (film)">Chitty Chitty Bang Bang</a></i>. Class-conscious themes also surface in works such as <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Mr_Fox" title="Fantastic Mr Fox">Fantastic Mr Fox</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Danny,_the_Champion_of_the_World" title="Danny, the Champion of the World">Danny, the Champion of the World</a></i> where the unpleasant wealthy neighbours are outwitted.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Studios_2016_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Studios_2016-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl also features in his books characters who are very fat, usually children. Augustus Gloop, Bruce Bogtrotter and Bruno Jenkins are a few of these characters, although an enormous woman named Aunt Sponge is featured in <i><a href="/wiki/James_and_the_Giant_Peach" title="James and the Giant Peach">James and the Giant Peach</a></i> and the nasty farmer Boggis in <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Mr_Fox" title="Fantastic Mr Fox">Fantastic Mr Fox</a></i> is an enormously fat character. All of these characters (with the possible exception of Bruce Bogtrotter) are either villains or simply unpleasant gluttons. They are usually punished for this: Augustus Gloop drinks from <a href="/wiki/Willy_Wonka" title="Willy Wonka">Willy Wonka</a>\'s chocolate river, disregarding the adults who tell him not to, and falls in, getting sucked up a pipe and nearly being turned into fudge. In <i><a href="/wiki/Matilda_(novel)" title="Matilda (novel)">Matilda</a></i>, Bruce Bogtrotter steals cake from the evil headmistress, Miss Trunchbull, and is forced to eat a gigantic chocolate cake in front of the school. Featured in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Witches_(book)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Witches (book)">The Witches</a></i>, Bruno Jenkins is lured by the witches into their convention with the promise of chocolate, before they turn him into a mouse.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Aunt Sponge is flattened by a giant peach. Dahl\'s mother used to tell him and his sisters tales about trolls and other mythical Norwegian creatures and some of his children\'s books contain references or elements inspired by these stories, such as the giants in <i><a href="/wiki/The_BFG" title="The BFG">The BFG</a></i>, the fox family in <i>Fantastic Mr Fox</i> and the trolls in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Minpins" title="The Minpins">The Minpins</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Receiving the 1983 <a href="/wiki/World_Fantasy_Award_for_Life_Achievement" class="mw-redirect" title="World Fantasy Award for Life Achievement">World Fantasy Award for Life Achievement</a>, Dahl encouraged his children and his readers to let their imagination run free. His daughter Lucy stated "his spirit was so large and so big he taught us to believe in magic."<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Studios_2016_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Studios_2016-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Those who don\'t believe in magic will never find it.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Roald Dahl, <i>The Minpins</i></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Dahl was also famous for his inventive, playful use of language, which was a key element to his writing. He would invent new words by scribbling down his words before swapping letters around and adopting <a href="/wiki/Spoonerisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Spoonerisms">spoonerisms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malapropisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Malapropisms">malapropisms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dahl_Dictionary_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dahl_Dictionary-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The lexicographer Dr Susan Rennie stated that Dahl built his new words on familiar sounds, adding:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He didn\'t always explain what his words meant, but children can work them out because they often sound like a word they know, and he loved using <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeia" title="Onomatopoeia">onomatopoeia</a>. For example, you know that something <i>lickswishy</i> and <i>delumptious</i> is good to eat, whereas something <i>uckyslush</i> or <i>rotsome</i> is not definitely not! He also used sounds that children love to say, like <i>squishous</i> and <i>squizzle</i>, or <i>fizzlecrump</i> and <i>fizzwiggler</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dahl_Dictionary_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dahl_Dictionary-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In 2016, marking the centenary of Dahl\'s birth, Rennie compiled <i>The Oxford Roald Dahl Dictionary</i> which includes many of his invented words and their meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-Dahl_Dictionary_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dahl_Dictionary-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Rennie commented that some of Dahl\'s words have already escaped his world, for example, <i>Scrumdiddlyumptious</i>: "Food that is utterly delicious".<sup id="cite_ref-Dahl_Dictionary_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dahl_Dictionary-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In his poetry, Dahl gives a humorous re-interpretation of well-known nursery rhymes and <a href="/wiki/Fairy_tales" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairy tales">fairy tales</a>, providing surprise endings in place of the traditional happily-ever-after. Dahl\'s collection of poems <i><a href="/wiki/Revolting_Rhymes" title="Revolting Rhymes">Revolting Rhymes</a></i> is recorded in <a href="/wiki/Audiobook" title="Audiobook">audiobook</a> form, and narrated by actor <a href="/wiki/Alan_Cumming" title="Alan Cumming">Alan Cumming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Screenplays">Screenplays</span></h3>\n<p>For a brief period in the 1960s, Dahl wrote screenplays. Two, the <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/You_Only_Live_Twice_(film)" title="You Only Live Twice (film)">You Only Live Twice</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Chitty_Chitty_Bang_Bang" title="Chitty Chitty Bang Bang">Chitty Chitty Bang Bang</a></i>, were adaptations of novels by <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming" title="Ian Fleming">Ian Fleming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Dahl also began adapting his own novel <i>Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</i>, which was completed and rewritten by <a href="/wiki/David_Seltzer" title="David Seltzer">David Seltzer</a> after Dahl failed to meet deadlines, and produced as the film <i><a href="/wiki/Willy_Wonka_%26_the_Chocolate_Factory" title="Willy Wonka &amp; the Chocolate Factory">Willy Wonka &amp; the Chocolate Factory</a></i> (1971). Dahl later disowned the film, saying he was "disappointed" because "he thought it placed too much emphasis on Willy Wonka and not enough on Charlie".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> He was also "infuriated" by the deviations in the plot devised by David Seltzer in his draft of the screenplay. This resulted in his refusal for any more versions of the book to be made in his lifetime, as well as an adaptation for the sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_and_the_Great_Glass_Elevator" title="Charlie and the Great Glass Elevator">Charlie and the Great Glass Elevator</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_(7896662300).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896662300%29.jpg/220px-Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896662300%29.jpg" width="220" height="295" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896662300%29.jpg/330px-Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896662300%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896662300%29.jpg/440px-Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896662300%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1936" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_(7896662300).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior of <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas" title="Dylan Thomas">Dylan Thomas</a>\'s writing shed. Dahl made a replica of it in his own garden in Great Missenden where he wrote many of his stories</div></div></div>\n<p>A major part of Dahl\'s literary influences stemmed from his childhood. In his younger days, he was an avid reader, especially awed by fantastic tales of heroism and triumph. Amongst his favourite authors were <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Makepeace_Thackeray" title="William Makepeace Thackeray">William Makepeace Thackeray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Marryat" title="Frederick Marryat">Frederick Marryat</a>, and their works went on to make a lasting mark on his life and writing.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Finding too many distractions in his house, Dahl remembered the poet <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas" title="Dylan Thomas">Dylan Thomas</a> had found a peaceful shed to write in close to home. Dahl travelled to visit Thomas\'s hut in Carmarthenshire, Wales in the 1950s and, after taking a look inside, decided to make a replica of it to write in.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dahl was also a huge fan of ghost stories and claimed that <i>Trolls</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Lie_(writer)" title="Jonas Lie (writer)">Jonas Lie</a> was one of the finest ghost stories ever written. While he was still a youngster, his mother, Sofie Dahl, would relate traditional Norwegian myths and legends from her native homeland to Dahl and his sisters. Dahl always maintained that his mother and her stories had a strong influence on his writing. In one interview, he mentioned: "She was a great teller of tales. Her memory was prodigious and nothing that ever happened to her in her life was forgotten."<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> When Dahl started writing and publishing his famous books for children, he created a grandmother character in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Witches_(book)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Witches (book)">The Witches</a></i> and later stated that she was based directly on his own mother as a tribute.<sup id="cite_ref-infloox_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infloox-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-infloox-1_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infloox-1-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span></h3>\n<p>In 1961, Dahl hosted and wrote for a <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horror_and_terror" title="Horror and terror">horror</a> television <a href="/wiki/Anthology_series" title="Anthology series">anthology series</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/Way_Out_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Way Out (TV series)">Way Out</a></i>, which preceded the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Twilight_Zone_(1959_TV_series)" title="The Twilight Zone (1959 TV series)">Twilight Zone</a></i> series on the <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> network for 14 episodes from March to July.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> One of the last dramatic network shows shot in New York City, the entire series is available for viewing at <a href="/wiki/The_Paley_Center_for_Media" class="mw-redirect" title="The Paley Center for Media">The Paley Center for Media</a> in New York City and Los Angeles.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> He also wrote for the satirical <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> comedy programme <i><a href="/wiki/That_Was_the_Week_That_Was" title="That Was the Week That Was">That Was the Week That Was</a></i>, which was hosted by <a href="/wiki/David_Frost" title="David Frost">David Frost</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British television series, <i><a href="/wiki/Tales_of_the_Unexpected_(TV_series)" title="Tales of the
